'To solve the inequality \\(x^{-1} > x\\), we first rewrite it in a more manageable form. The expression \\(x^{-1}\\) is equivalent to \\(\\frac{1}{x}\\), so the inequality becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{x} > x\n\\]\n\nMultiplying both sides by \\(x\\) (assuming \\(x > 0\\) since we are looking for positive integer values), we get:\n\n\\[\n1 > x^2\n\\]\n\nThis implies:\n\n\\[\nx^2 < 1\n\\]\n\nSince \\(x\\) is a positive integer, the only possible value for \\(x\\) that satisfies \\(x^2 < 1\\) is \\(x = 1\\).\n\nTherefore, there is only one positive integer value of \\(x\\) for which \\(x^{-1} > x\\), and that is \\(x = 1\\).\n\nThus, the number of positive integer values of \\(x\\) is \\(\\boxed{1}\\).'
'To find \\( a + b \\), we need to use the conditions given for the function \\( f(x) \\).\n\n1. **Condition at \\( x = -4 \\):**\n\n   \\[\n   f(-4) = \\frac{a}{b}(-4) = -\\frac{60}{13}\n   \\]\n\n   Solving for \\( \\frac{a}{b} \\):\n\n   \\[\n   \\frac{a}{b} \\cdot (-4) = -\\frac{60}{13} \\implies \\frac{a}{b} = \\frac{60}{52} = \\frac{15}{13}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Condition at \\( x = 4 \\):**\n\n   \\[\n   f(4) = ab(4)^2 = 3120\n   \\]\n\n   Solving for \\( ab \\):\n\n   \\[\n   ab \\cdot 16 = 3120 \\implies ab = \\frac{3120}{16} = 195\n   \\]\n\nNow, we have two equations:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{a}{b} = \\frac{15}{13}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nab = 195\n\\]\n\nFrom the first equation, express \\( a \\) in terms of \\( b \\):\n\n\\[\na = \\frac{15}{13}b\n\\]\n\nSubstitute into the second equation:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{15}{13}b\\right)b = 195\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\frac{15}{13}b^2 = 195\n\\]\n\nMultiply both sides by 13:\n\n\\[\n15b^2 = 195 \\times 13\n\\]\n\nCalculate \\( 195 \\times 13 \\):\n\n\\[\n195 \\times 13 = 2535\n\\]\n\nSo:\n\n\\[\n15b^2 = 2535\n\\]\n\nDivide by 15:\n\n\\[\nb^2 = \\frac{2535}{15} = 169\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root:\n\n\\[\nb = \\sqrt{169} = 13\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( b = 13 \\) back to find \\( a \\):\n\n\\[\na = \\frac{15}{13} \\times 13 = 15\n\\]\n\nFinally, calculate \\( a + b \\):\n\n\\[\na + b = 15 + 13 = \\boxed{28}\n\\]'
'To find when the ball hits the ground, we need to determine when the height \\( y \\) is zero. We set the equation \\( y = -16t^2 - 60t + 54 \\) equal to zero and solve for \\( t \\):\n\n\\[\n-16t^2 - 60t + 54 = 0\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in the form \\( at^2 + bt + c = 0 \\), where \\( a = -16 \\), \\( b = -60 \\), and \\( c = 54 \\). We can use the quadratic formula to solve for \\( t \\):\n\n\\[\nt = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the values of \\( a \\), \\( b \\), and \\( c \\) into the formula:\n\n\\[\nt = \\frac{-(-60) \\pm \\sqrt{(-60)^2 - 4(-16)(54)}}{2(-16)}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nt = \\frac{60 \\pm \\sqrt{3600 + 3456}}{-32}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nt = \\frac{60 \\pm \\sqrt{7056}}{-32}\n\\]\n\nCalculate the square root of 7056:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{7056} = 84\n\\]\n\nSubstitute back into the equation:\n\n\\[\nt = \\frac{60 \\pm 84}{-32}\n\\]\n\nThis gives us two possible solutions for \\( t \\):\n\n1. \\( t = \\frac{60 + 84}{-32} = \\frac{144}{-32} = -4.5 \\)\n2. \\( t = \\frac{60 - 84}{-32} = \\frac{-24}{-32} = 0.75 \\)\n\nSince time cannot be negative, we discard \\( t = -4.5 \\). Therefore, the ball hits the ground at \\( t = 0.75 \\) seconds.\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{0.75}\\).'
'To evaluate \\(\\lfloor 14.6 \\rfloor - \\lceil -14.6 \\rceil\\), we need to find the floor and ceiling of the given numbers.\n\n1. \\(\\lfloor 14.6 \\rfloor\\) is the greatest integer less than or equal to 14.6. This is 14.\n\n2. \\(\\lceil -14.6 \\rceil\\) is the smallest integer greater than or equal to -14.6. This is -14.\n\nNow, we compute the expression:\n\n\\[\n\\lfloor 14.6 \\rfloor - \\lceil -14.6 \\rceil = 14 - (-14) = 14 + 14 = 28\n\\]\n\nThus, the result is \\(\\boxed{28}\\).'
'To find the percent increase in the area of the TV screen, we first calculate the original area and the new area after increasing each dimension by 20%.\n\n1. **Original Area:**\n   \\[\n   \\text{Original Area} = 24 \\times 16 = 384 \\text{ square inches}\n   \\]\n\n2. **New Dimensions:**\n   - The length is increased by 20%, so the new length is:\n     \\[\n     24 + 0.2 \\times 24 = 24 \\times 1.2 = 28.8 \\text{ inches}\n     \\]\n   - The width is increased by 20%, so the new width is:\n     \\[\n     16 + 0.2 \\times 16 = 16 \\times 1.2 = 19.2 \\text{ inches}\n     \\]\n\n3. **New Area:**\n   \\[\n   \\text{New Area} = 28.8 \\times 19.2 = 552.96 \\text{ square inches}\n   \\]\n\n4. **Percent Increase in Area:**\n   - The increase in area is:\n     \\[\n     552.96 - 384 = 168.96 \\text{ square inches}\n     \\]\n   - The percent increase is:\n     \\[\n     \\left(\\frac{168.96}{384}\\right) \\times 100\\% \\approx 44\\%\n     \\]\n\nTherefore, the area is increased by approximately \\( \\boxed{44\\%} \\).'
'To expand the expression \\((q-5)(3r+6)\\), we use the distributive property, also known as the FOIL method for binomials. This involves multiplying each term in the first binomial by each term in the second binomial:\n\n1. Multiply \\(q\\) by \\(3r\\): \\(q \\cdot 3r = 3qr\\).\n2. Multiply \\(q\\) by \\(6\\): \\(q \\cdot 6 = 6q\\).\n3. Multiply \\(-5\\) by \\(3r\\): \\(-5 \\cdot 3r = -15r\\).\n4. Multiply \\(-5\\) by \\(6\\): \\(-5 \\cdot 6 = -30\\).\n\nNow, combine all these terms:\n\n\\[3qr + 6q - 15r - 30\\]\n\nSo, the expanded form of \\((q-5)(3r+6)\\) is:\n\n\\[3qr + 6q - 15r - 30\\]'
'To solve this problem, we need to calculate the total distance traveled by the ball after it hits the floor for the sixth time. The ball is dropped from a height of 16 feet and each time it bounces back to half the height from which it fell.\n\n1. **First drop and bounce:**\n   - The ball falls 16 feet.\n   - It bounces back to a height of \\( \\frac{16}{2} = 8 \\) feet.\n\n2. **Second drop and bounce:**\n   - The ball falls 8 feet.\n   - It bounces back to a height of \\( \\frac{8}{2} = 4 \\) feet.\n\n3. **Third drop and bounce:**\n   - The ball falls 4 feet.\n   - It bounces back to a height of \\( \\frac{4}{2} = 2 \\) feet.\n\n4. **Fourth drop and bounce:**\n   - The ball falls 2 feet.\n   - It bounces back to a height of \\( \\frac{2}{2} = 1 \\) foot.\n\n5. **Fifth drop and bounce:**\n   - The ball falls 1 foot.\n   - It bounces back to a height of \\( \\frac{1}{2} = 0.5 \\) feet.\n\n6. **Sixth drop:**\n   - The ball falls 0.5 feet.\n\nNow, we sum up all the distances:\n\n- The initial drop is 16 feet.\n- The bounces and subsequent drops are: \\( 8 + 8 + 4 + 4 + 2 + 2 + 1 + 1 + 0.5 = 27.5 \\) feet.\n\nAdding the initial drop, the total distance traveled by the ball is:\n\n\\[ 16 + 27.5 = 43.5 \\text{ feet} \\]\n\nTherefore, the ball will have traveled a total of 43.5 feet when it hits the floor for the sixth time.'
"To find the maximum possible value for \\( x \\), we start by using the equation of the circle centered at \\( (5, 15) \\) with radius \\( \\sqrt{130} \\):\n\n\\[\n(x - 5)^2 + (y - 15)^2 = 130\n\\]\n\nGiven that the point \\( Q = (x, y) \\) is on the circle and \\( x = 2y \\), we substitute \\( x = 2y \\) into the circle's equation:\n\n\\[\n(2y - 5)^2 + (y - 15)^2 = 130\n\\]\n\nExpanding both squares, we have:\n\n\\[\n(4y^2 - 20y + 25) + (y^2 - 30y + 225) = 130\n\\]\n\nCombine like terms:\n\n\\[\n4y^2 + y^2 - 20y - 30y + 25 + 225 = 130\n\\]\n\n\\[\n5y^2 - 50y + 250 = 130\n\\]\n\nSubtract 130 from both sides:\n\n\\[\n5y^2 - 50y + 120 = 0\n\\]\n\nDivide the entire equation by 5 to simplify:\n\n\\[\ny^2 - 10y + 24 = 0\n\\]\n\nNow, solve the quadratic equation using the quadratic formula \\( y = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} \\), where \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = -10 \\), and \\( c = 24 \\):\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{-(-10) \\pm \\sqrt{(-10)^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot 24}}{2 \\cdot 1}\n\\]\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{10 \\pm \\sqrt{100 - 96}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{10 \\pm \\sqrt{4}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{10 \\pm 2}{2}\n\\]\n\nThis gives us two possible values for \\( y \\):\n\n\\[\ny = \\frac{12}{2} = 6 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad y = \\frac{8}{2} = 4\n\\]\n\nSince \\( x = 2y \\), we calculate \\( x \\) for both values of \\( y \\):\n\n- If \\( y = 6 \\), then \\( x = 2 \\times 6 = 12 \\).\n- If \\( y = 4 \\), then \\( x = 2 \\times 4 = 8 \\).\n\nThe maximum possible value for \\( x \\) is therefore \\( \\boxed{12} \\)."
'To find the integer that lies between \\(\\sqrt[3]{-45}\\) and \\(\\sqrt[3]{-101}\\), we first need to approximate these cube roots.\n\n1. **Approximate \\(\\sqrt[3]{-45}\\):**\n\n   We know that \\(-3^3 = -27\\) and \\(-4^3 = -64\\). Since \\(-45\\) is between \\(-27\\) and \\(-64\\), \\(\\sqrt[3]{-45}\\) is between \\(-3\\) and \\(-4\\). Since \\(-45\\) is closer to \\(-64\\) than to \\(-27\\), \\(\\sqrt[3]{-45}\\) is closer to \\(-3.5\\).\n\n2. **Approximate \\(\\sqrt[3]{-101}\\):**\n\n   We know that \\(-4^3 = -64\\) and \\(-5^3 = -125\\). Since \\(-101\\) is between \\(-64\\) and \\(-125\\), \\(\\sqrt[3]{-101}\\) is between \\(-4\\) and \\(-5\\). Since \\(-101\\) is closer to \\(-125\\) than to \\(-64\\), \\(\\sqrt[3]{-101}\\) is closer to \\(-4.5\\).\n\nGiven these approximations, \\(\\sqrt[3]{-45} \\approx -3.5\\) and \\(\\sqrt[3]{-101} \\approx -4.5\\). The integer that lies between \\(-3.5\\) and \\(-4.5\\) is \\(-4\\).\n\nTherefore, the integer that lies between \\(\\sqrt[3]{-45}\\) and \\(\\sqrt[3]{-101}\\) is \\(\\boxed{-4}\\).'

'To find the value of \\( x \\) for which \\( f(g(x)) = g(f(x)) \\), we first need to compute each composition.\n\nFirst, compute \\( f(g(x)) \\):\n\\[ g(x) = x^2 \\]\n\\[ f(g(x)) = f(x^2) = x^2 + 2 \\]\n\nNext, compute \\( g(f(x)) \\):\n\\[ f(x) = x + 2 \\]\n\\[ g(f(x)) = g(x + 2) = (x + 2)^2 = x^2 + 4x + 4 \\]\n\nNow, set the two expressions equal to each other:\n\\[ x^2 + 2 = x^2 + 4x + 4 \\]\n\nSubtract \\( x^2 \\) from both sides:\n\\[ 2 = 4x + 4 \\]\n\nSubtract 4 from both sides:\n\\[ -2 = 4x \\]\n\nDivide both sides by 4:\n\\[ x = -\\frac{1}{2} \\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( x \\) for which \\( f(g(x)) = g(f(x)) \\) is \\(\\boxed{-\\frac{1}{2}}\\).'
'To evaluate \\(\\log_{5^2}5^4\\), we can use the change of base formula and properties of logarithms. \n\nFirst, recognize that \\(\\log_{5^2}5^4\\) can be rewritten using the property of logarithms that states \\(\\log_{a^b}c^d = \\frac{d}{b} \\log_a c\\). In this case, \\(a = 5\\), \\(b = 2\\), \\(c = 5\\), and \\(d = 4\\).\n\nThus, we have:\n\\[\n\\log_{5^2}5^4 = \\frac{4}{2} \\log_5 5\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\log_5 5 = 1\\) (because any logarithm of a number to its own base is 1), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{2} \\cdot 1 = 2\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\log_{5^2}5^4 = \\boxed{2}\\).'
'To find the \\( y \\)-intercepts of the graph of the parabola given by the equation \\( x = y^2 - 4y - 1 \\), we need to set \\( x = 0 \\) and solve for \\( y \\).\n\nThe equation becomes:\n\\[\n0 = y^2 - 4y - 1\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in \\( y \\). We can solve it using the quadratic formula:\n\\[\ny = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\n\\]\nwhere \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = -4 \\), and \\( c = -1 \\).\n\nFirst, calculate the discriminant:\n\\[\nb^2 - 4ac = (-4)^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot (-1) = 16 + 4 = 20\n\\]\n\nSince the discriminant is positive, there are two distinct real solutions for \\( y \\).\n\nNow, apply the quadratic formula:\n\\[\ny = \\frac{-(-4) \\pm \\sqrt{20}}{2 \\cdot 1} = \\frac{4 \\pm \\sqrt{20}}{2}\n\\]\n\nSimplify \\(\\sqrt{20}\\):\n\\[\n\\sqrt{20} = \\sqrt{4 \\cdot 5} = 2\\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute back:\n\\[\ny = \\frac{4 \\pm 2\\sqrt{5}}{2} = 2 \\pm \\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\nThus, the solutions are \\( y = 2 + \\sqrt{5} \\) and \\( y = 2 - \\sqrt{5} \\).\n\nTherefore, the graph of the parabola has two \\( y \\)-intercepts. The answer is \\(\\boxed{2}\\).'
"To rewrite the expression \\(6y^2 - y - 51\\) in the form \\((3Ay + B)(y - C)\\), we need to factor the quadratic expression.\n\nFirst, let's factor \\(6y^2 - y - 51\\). We are looking for two numbers whose product is \\(6 \\times (-51) = -306\\) and whose sum is \\(-1\\).\n\nThe numbers that satisfy these conditions are \\(17\\) and \\(-18\\) because \\(17 \\times (-18) = -306\\) and \\(17 + (-18) = -1\\).\n\nNow, we can rewrite the middle term \\(-y\\) using these numbers:\n\\[\n6y^2 - y - 51 = 6y^2 + 17y - 18y - 51\n\\]\n\nNext, we group the terms and factor by grouping:\n\\[\n= (6y^2 + 17y) + (-18y - 51)\n\\]\n\nFactor out the greatest common factor from each group:\n\\[\n= y(6y + 17) - 3(6y + 17)\n\\]\n\nNow, factor out the common binomial factor:\n\\[\n= (y - 3)(6y + 17)\n\\]\n\nWe need to express this in the form \\((3Ay + B)(y - C)\\). Notice that:\n\\[\n(y - 3)(6y + 17) = (3 \\cdot 2y + 17)(y - 3)\n\\]\n\nThus, we have \\(A = 2\\), \\(B = 17\\), and \\(C = 3\\).\n\nNow, calculate \\((AC)^2 - B\\):\n\\[\nAC = 2 \\times 3 = 6\n\\]\n\\[\n(AC)^2 = 6^2 = 36\n\\]\n\\[\n(AC)^2 - B = 36 - 17 = 19\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\((AC)^2 - B\\) is \\(\\boxed{19}\\)."
'To rewrite the expression \\(5x - |x - 5|\\) without using absolute value signs, we need to consider the condition given: \\(x < 5\\).\n\nThe absolute value expression \\(|x - 5|\\) can be rewritten based on the value of \\(x\\) relative to 5. Specifically:\n\n- If \\(x \\geq 5\\), then \\(|x - 5| = x - 5\\).\n- If \\(x < 5\\), then \\(|x - 5| = -(x - 5) = 5 - x\\).\n\nSince we are given that \\(x < 5\\), we use the second case:\n\n\\[\n|x - 5| = 5 - x\n\\]\n\nSubstituting this into the original expression, we have:\n\n\\[\n5x - |x - 5| = 5x - (5 - x) = 5x - 5 + x = 6x - 5\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression \\(5x - |x - 5|\\) can be rewritten as \\(6x - 5\\) for \\(x < 5\\).'

We know that:\n\n\\[ 32 = 2^5. \\]\n\nThus, the equation becomes:\n\n\\[ 2^{x + 2} = 2^5. \\]\n\nSince the bases are the same, we can equate the exponents:\n\n\\[ x + 2 = 5. \\]\n\nSolving for \\(x\\), we subtract 2 from both sides:\n\n\\[ x = 5 - 2 = 3. \\]\n\nTherefore, the integer \\(x\\) that satisfies the equation is \\(\\boxed{3}\\).'

"To solve this problem, we need to establish the relationships between the costs of cookies, brownies, and cupcakes.\n\n1. **Cookies and Brownies:**\n   - Six cookies cost the same as 2 brownies.\n   - Therefore, the cost of 1 brownie is equivalent to the cost of 3 cookies.\n\n2. **Brownies and Cupcakes:**\n   - Four brownies cost the same as 10 cupcakes.\n   - Therefore, the cost of 1 brownie is equivalent to the cost of \\( \\frac{10}{4} = 2.5 \\) cupcakes.\n\nNow, let's find out how many cupcakes Bob can buy for the price of eighteen cookies:\n\n- Since 1 brownie costs the same as 3 cookies, the cost of 18 cookies is equivalent to the cost of \\( \\frac{18}{3} = 6 \\) brownies.\n\n- Since 1 brownie costs the same as 2.5 cupcakes, the cost of 6 brownies is equivalent to the cost of \\( 6 \\times 2.5 = 15 \\) cupcakes.\n\nTherefore, Bob can buy 15 cupcakes for the price of eighteen cookies. 'To find the maximum value of the quadratic function \\( f(x) = -x^2 - 8x + 12 \\), we can use the vertex formula for a parabola. The function is in the form \\( f(x) = ax^2 + bx + c \\), where \\( a = -1 \\), \\( b = -8 \\), and \\( c = 12 \\).\n\nThe vertex of a parabola given by \\( ax^2 + bx + c \\) is at \\( x = -\\frac{b}{2a} \\). Substituting the values of \\( a \\) and \\( b \\):\n\n\\[\nx = -\\frac{-8}{2 \\times -1} = \\frac{8}{-2} = -4\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute \\( x = -4 \\) back into the function to find the maximum value:\n\n\\[\nf(-4) = -(-4)^2 - 8(-4) + 12\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -16 + 32 + 12\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 28\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the maximum value of the function \\( -x^2 - 8x + 12 \\) is \\(\\boxed{28}\\).'
"To find the value of \\(\\frac{d}{c}\\), we need to solve the system of equations given:\n\n1. \\(d - 6c = 4\\)\n2. \\(2d - 9c = 20\\)\n\nWe can solve this system using the method of elimination. First, let's eliminate \\(d\\) by making the coefficients of \\(d\\) in both equations equal. We can do this by multiplying the first equation by 2:\n\n\\[\n2(d - 6c) = 2 \\times 4\n\\]\n\nThis gives us:\n\n\\[\n2d - 12c = 8\n\\]\n\nNow we have the system:\n\n1. \\(2d - 12c = 8\\)\n2. \\(2d - 9c = 20\\)\n\nSubtract the first equation from the second equation to eliminate \\(d\\):\n\n\\[\n(2d - 9c) - (2d - 12c) = 20 - 8\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n-9c + 12c = 12\n\\]\n\n\\[\n3c = 12\n\\]\n\n\\[\nc = 4\n\\]\n\nNow that we have \\(c = 4\\), we can substitute this value back into the first equation to find \\(d\\):\n\n\\[\nd - 6(4) = 4\n\\]\n\n\\[\nd - 24 = 4\n\\]\n\n\\[\nd = 28\n\\]\n\nNow we have \\(d = 28\\) and \\(c = 4\\). We can find \\(\\frac{d}{c}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\frac{d}{c} = \\frac{28}{4} = 7\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\frac{d}{c}\\) is \\(\\boxed{7}\\)."
'To solve the problem, we need to find the values of \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\) that satisfy the given equations and then compute \\(c + \\frac{1}{a}\\).\n\nFirst, consider the equation:\n\n\\[\na + \\frac{1}{b} = \\frac{22}{7}\n\\]\n\nRewriting it, we have:\n\n\\[\na = \\frac{22}{7} - \\frac{1}{b}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(a\\) is an integer, \\(\\frac{22}{7} - \\frac{1}{b}\\) must also be an integer. Let \\(a = 3\\), then:\n\n\\[\n3 = \\frac{22}{7} - \\frac{1}{b} \\implies \\frac{1}{b} = \\frac{22}{7} - 3 = \\frac{22}{7} - \\frac{21}{7} = \\frac{1}{7}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(b = 7\\).\n\nNext, consider the equation:\n\n\\[\nb + \\frac{1}{c} = 8\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(b = 7\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n7 + \\frac{1}{c} = 8 \\implies \\frac{1}{c} = 1 \\implies c = 1\n\\]\n\nNow, we have \\(a = 3\\), \\(b = 7\\), and \\(c = 1\\). We need to verify that these values satisfy the condition \\(abc = 21\\):\n\n\\[\nabc = 3 \\times 7 \\times 1 = 21\n\\]\n\nThis condition is satisfied. Finally, we need to find \\(c + \\frac{1}{a}\\):\n\n\\[\nc + \\frac{1}{a} = 1 + \\frac{1}{3} = \\frac{3}{3} + \\frac{1}{3} = \\frac{4}{3}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(c + \\frac{1}{a}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{4}{3}}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to find the greatest three-digit number "abc" such that the digits \\(4, a, b\\) form a geometric sequence and the digits \\(b, c, 5\\) form an arithmetic sequence.\n\n1. **Geometric Sequence: \\(4, a, b\\)**\n\n   In a geometric sequence, the ratio between consecutive terms is constant. Let the common ratio be \\(r\\). Then:\n   \\[\n   a = 4r \\quad \\text{and} \\quad b = 4r^2\n   \\]\n\n2. **Arithmetic Sequence: \\(b, c, 5\\)**\n\n   In an arithmetic sequence, the difference between consecutive terms is constant. Let the common difference be \\(d\\). Then:\n   \\[\n   c = b + d \\quad \\text{and} \\quad 5 = c + d\n   \\]\n\n   From the second equation, we have:\n   \\[\n   d = 5 - c\n   \\]\n\n   Substituting into the first equation:\n   \\[\n   c = b + (5 - c) \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad 2c = b + 5 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad c = \\frac{b + 5}{2}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Combining the Sequences**\n\n   We have:\n   \\[\n   b = 4r^2 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad c = \\frac{4r^2 + 5}{2}\n   \\]\n\n   Since \\(a, b, c\\) are digits (0 through 9), we need to find integer values of \\(r\\) such that \\(a, b, c\\) are digits.\n\n4. **Finding the Greatest Number**\n\n   We want to maximize the number "abc". Start by testing the largest possible values for \\(r\\) that keep \\(a, b, c\\) as digits.\n\n   - If \\(r = 1\\):\n     \\[\n     a = 4 \\times 1 = 4, \\quad b = 4 \\times 1^2 = 4, \\quad c = \\frac{4 + 5}{2} = 4.5 \\quad (\\text{not an integer})\n     \\]\n\n   - If \\(r = \\frac{3}{2}\\):\n     \\[\n     a = 4 \\times \\frac{3}{2} = 6, \\quad b = 4 \\times \\left(\\frac{3}{2}\\right)^2 = 9, \\quad c = \\frac{9 + 5}{2} = 7\n     \\]\n\n     This gives us the number "abc" = 697, which is valid.\n\n   - If \\(r = 2\\):\n     \\[\n     a = 4 \\times 2 = 8, \\quad b = 4 \\times 2^2 = 16 \\quad (\\text{not a digit})\n     \\]\n\n   Therefore, the greatest three-digit number "abc" that satisfies the conditions is \\(\\boxed{697}\\).'
'To rewrite the quadratic \\(x^2 - 6x + 66\\) in the form \\((x+b)^2 + c\\), we need to complete the square.\n\nStart with the quadratic expression:\n\\[ x^2 - 6x + 66. \\]\n\nTo complete the square, focus on the \\(x^2 - 6x\\) part. Take half of the coefficient of \\(x\\), which is \\(-6\\), and square it:\n\\[ \\left(\\frac{-6}{2}\\right)^2 = 9. \\]\n\nAdd and subtract this square inside the expression:\n\\[ x^2 - 6x + 9 - 9 + 66. \\]\n\nThis can be rewritten as:\n\\[ (x - 3)^2 - 9 + 66. \\]\n\nSimplify the constant terms:\n\\[ (x - 3)^2 + 57. \\]\n\nNow, the quadratic is in the form \\((x+b)^2 + c\\), where \\(b = -3\\) and \\(c = 57\\).\n\nThus, \\(b + c = -3 + 57 = 54\\).\n\nThe value of \\(b + c\\) is \\(\\boxed{54}\\).'

'To solve the inequality \\(x^2 - 5x - 4 \\le 10\\), we first rewrite it as:\n\n\\[ x^2 - 5x - 4 - 10 \\le 0 \\]\n\nwhich simplifies to:\n\n\\[ x^2 - 5x - 14 \\le 0 \\]\n\nNext, we solve the corresponding equation:\n\n\\[ x^2 - 5x - 14 = 0 \\]\n\nTo find the roots, we use the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[ x = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} \\]\n\nwhere \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = -5\\), and \\(c = -14\\). Plugging in these values, we get:\n\n\\[ x = \\frac{-(-5) \\pm \\sqrt{(-5)^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot (-14)}}{2 \\cdot 1} \\]\n\n\\[ x = \\frac{5 \\pm \\sqrt{25 + 56}}{2} \\]\n\n\\[ x = \\frac{5 \\pm \\sqrt{81}}{2} \\]\n\n\\[ x = \\frac{5 \\pm 9}{2} \\]\n\nThis gives us the roots:\n\n\\[ x = \\frac{5 + 9}{2} = 7 \\]\n\n\\[ x = \\frac{5 - 9}{2} = -2 \\]\n\nThe roots of the equation are \\(x = -2\\) and \\(x = 7\\). These roots divide the number line into three intervals: \\((-\\infty, -2)\\), \\([-2, 7]\\), and \\((7, \\infty)\\).\n\nTo determine where the inequality \\(x^2 - 5x - 14 \\le 0\\) holds, we test a point in each interval:\n\n1. For the interval \\((-\\infty, -2)\\), choose \\(x = -3\\):\n   \\[ (-3)^2 - 5(-3) - 14 = 9 + 15 - 14 = 10 \\]\n   Since \\(10 > 0\\), the inequality does not hold in this interval.\n\n2. For the interval \\([-2, 7]\\), choose \\(x = 0\\):\n   \\[ 0^2 - 5(0) - 14 = -14 \\]\n   Since \\(-14 \\le 0\\), the inequality holds in this interval.\n\n3. For the interval \\((7, \\infty)\\), choose \\(x = 8\\):\n   \\[ 8^2 - 5(8) - 14 = 64 - 40 - 14 = 10 \\]\n   Since \\(10 > 0\\), the inequality does not hold in this interval.\n\nThus, the solution to the inequality \\(x^2 - 5x - 14 \\le 0\\) is the interval \\([-2, 7]\\).\n\nTherefore, the values of \\(x\\) for which the inequality holds are:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{[-2, 7]}\n\\]'
'To solve for \\( A + M + C \\), we start by expressing the numbers \\( AMC10 \\) and \\( AMC12 \\) in terms of their digits:\n\n\\[\nAMC10 = 10000A + 1000M + 100C + 10 \\times 1 + 0 = 10000A + 1000M + 100C + 10\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAMC12 = 10000A + 1000M + 100C + 10 \\times 1 + 2 = 10000A + 1000M + 100C + 12\n\\]\n\nThe sum of these two numbers is given as:\n\n\\[\nAMC10 + AMC12 = 123422\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the expressions for \\( AMC10 \\) and \\( AMC12 \\):\n\n\\[\n(10000A + 1000M + 100C + 10) + (10000A + 1000M + 100C + 12) = 123422\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the left side:\n\n\\[\n20000A + 2000M + 200C + 22 = 123422\n\\]\n\nSubtract 22 from both sides:\n\n\\[\n20000A + 2000M + 200C = 123400\n\\]\n\nDivide the entire equation by 200:\n\n\\[\n100A + 10M + C = 617\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find the digits \\( A \\), \\( M \\), and \\( C \\) such that the equation holds. We can try different values for \\( A \\) to find a valid combination:\n\n1. If \\( A = 6 \\):\n\n   \\[\n   100 \\times 6 + 10M + C = 617\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   600 + 10M + C = 617\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   10M + C = 17\n   \\]\n\n   Possible values for \\( M \\) and \\( C \\) are \\( M = 1 \\) and \\( C = 7 \\).\n\nThus, \\( A = 6 \\), \\( M = 1 \\), and \\( C = 7 \\).\n\nFinally, calculate \\( A + M + C \\):\n\n\\[\nA + M + C = 6 + 1 + 7 = 14\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\( A + M + C \\) is \\(\\boxed{14}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we first determine the total amount of work required to mow the lawn in terms of person-hours. If five people can mow the lawn in 12 hours, the total work is:\n\n\\[ 5 \\text{ people} \\times 12 \\text{ hours} = 60 \\text{ person-hours}. \\]\n\nNow, we want to find out how many people are needed to complete the same amount of work in just 3 hours. Let \\( n \\) be the number of people needed. The equation for the total work is:\n\n\\[ n \\text{ people} \\times 3 \\text{ hours} = 60 \\text{ person-hours}. \\]\n\nSolving for \\( n \\), we have:\n\n\\[ n \\times 3 = 60 \\]\n\n\\[ n = \\frac{60}{3} = 20. \\]\n\nTherefore, 20 people are needed to mow the lawn in 3 hours. Since we already have 5 people, the number of additional people needed is:\n\n\\[ 20 - 5 = 15. \\]\n\nThus, 15 more people are needed to mow the lawn in 3 hours.'
'To find \\( x^3 - y^3 \\), we can use the identity:\n\n\\[\nx^3 - y^3 = (x-y)(x^2 + xy + y^2)\n\\]\n\nWe are given \\( x-y = 6 \\). We need to find \\( x^2 + xy + y^2 \\).\n\nWe know:\n\n\\[\nx^2 + y^2 = 24\n\\]\n\nWe can express \\( x^2 + xy + y^2 \\) in terms of \\( x^2 + y^2 \\) and \\( xy \\):\n\n\\[\nx^2 + xy + y^2 = (x^2 + y^2) + xy\n\\]\n\nTo find \\( xy \\), we use the identity:\n\n\\[\n(x-y)^2 = x^2 - 2xy + y^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given values:\n\n\\[\n6^2 = x^2 - 2xy + y^2\n\\]\n\n\\[\n36 = x^2 - 2xy + y^2\n\\]\n\nWe know \\( x^2 + y^2 = 24 \\), so:\n\n\\[\n36 = 24 - 2xy\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( xy \\):\n\n\\[\n36 = 24 - 2xy \\implies 2xy = 24 - 36 = -12 \\implies xy = -6\n\\]\n\nNow substitute back to find \\( x^2 + xy + y^2 \\):\n\n\\[\nx^2 + xy + y^2 = 24 + (-6) = 18\n\\]\n\nFinally, substitute into the expression for \\( x^3 - y^3 \\):\n\n\\[\nx^3 - y^3 = (x-y)(x^2 + xy + y^2) = 6 \\times 18 = 108\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( x^3 - y^3 \\) is \\(\\boxed{108}\\).'
'To evaluate \\(\\sqrt[3]{12} \\times \\sqrt[3]{20} \\times \\sqrt[3]{15} \\times \\sqrt[3]{60}\\), we can use the property of cube roots that states \\(\\sqrt[3]{a} \\times \\sqrt[3]{b} = \\sqrt[3]{a \\times b}\\).\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt[3]{12} \\times \\sqrt[3]{20} \\times \\sqrt[3]{15} \\times \\sqrt[3]{60} = \\sqrt[3]{12 \\times 20 \\times 15 \\times 60}\n\\]\n\nNow, we calculate the product inside the cube root:\n\n\\[\n12 \\times 20 \\times 15 \\times 60\n\\]\n\nFirst, break down each number into its prime factors:\n\n- \\(12 = 2^2 \\times 3\\)\n- \\(20 = 2^2 \\times 5\\)\n- \\(15 = 3 \\times 5\\)\n- \\(60 = 2^2 \\times 3 \\times 5\\)\n\nNow, multiply these together:\n\n\\[\n(2^2 \\times 3) \\times (2^2 \\times 5) \\times (3 \\times 5) \\times (2^2 \\times 3 \\times 5)\n\\]\n\nCombine all the factors:\n\n\\[\n2^{2+2+2} \\times 3^{1+1+1} \\times 5^{1+1+1} = 2^6 \\times 3^3 \\times 5^3\n\\]\n\nNow, take the cube root:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt[3]{2^6 \\times 3^3 \\times 5^3} = 2^{6/3} \\times 3^{3/3} \\times 5^{3/3} = 2^2 \\times 3 \\times 5\n\\]\n\nCalculate the result:\n\n\\[\n2^2 = 4, \\quad 3 = 3, \\quad 5 = 5\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\n\\[\n4 \\times 3 \\times 5 = 12 \\times 5 = 60\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\sqrt[3]{12} \\times \\sqrt[3]{20} \\times \\sqrt[3]{15} \\times \\sqrt[3]{60}\\) is \\(\\boxed{60}\\).'
"Bob's rate of travel is \\(\\frac{m}{h}\\) miles per hour. To find out how many hours it would take him to travel \\(h\\) miles, we set up the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Time} = \\frac{\\text{Distance}}{\\text{Rate}} = \\frac{h}{\\frac{m}{h}}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the expression, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{h}{\\frac{m}{h}} = \\frac{h \\cdot h}{m} = \\frac{h^2}{m}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, it would take Bob \\(\\frac{h^2}{m}\\) hours to travel \\(h\\) miles. The answer is \\(\\frac{h^2}{m}\\)."
"To find Cal O'Ree's expected weight at the end of ten weeks, we need to calculate his weight loss each week as 1% of his weight at the end of the previous week. This is a compound percentage problem.\n\n1. **Initial Weight**: 244 pounds.\n\n2. **Weekly Weight Loss**: 1% of the previous week's weight.\n\n3. **Weight Calculation**: Each week, Cal's weight is 99% of the previous week's weight, because he loses 1%.\n\nThe formula for his weight at the end of \\( n \\) weeks is:\n\\[ W_n = W_0 \\times (0.99)^n \\]\nwhere \\( W_0 \\) is the initial weight, and \\( n \\) is the number of weeks.\n\nFor Cal, \\( W_0 = 244 \\) pounds and \\( n = 10 \\).\n\n\\[ W_{10} = 244 \\times (0.99)^{10} \\]\n\nNow, calculate \\( (0.99)^{10} \\):\n\n\\[ (0.99)^{10} \\approx 0.904382075 \\]\n\nThen, calculate \\( W_{10} \\):\n\n\\[ W_{10} = 244 \\times 0.904382075 \\approx 220.668 \\]\n\nRounding to the nearest whole number, Cal's expected weight at the end of ten weeks is approximately \\( \\boxed{221} \\) pounds."
